<p><a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> sex offense victims in 2012:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>67,354 female</li>
<li>12,000 male.</li>
</ul>
<p>FBI convicted sex offenders in 2012:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>78,500 male</li>
<li>4394 female.</li>
</ul>
<p>Statistics maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> include:</p>
<ul>
<li>27% of women and 12% of men have experienced sexual violence, including rape</li>
<li>50% of bisexual women have been raped at least once in their life.</li>
<li>12.5% of lesbians have been raped at least once in their life.</li>
<li>17% of heterosexual women have been raped at least once in their life.</li>
<li>50% of bisexual men have experienced sexual violence other than rape in their lifetime</li>
<li>50% of gay men have experienced sexual violence other than rape in their lifetime</li>
<li>20% of heterosexual men have experienced sexual violence other than rape in their lifetime</li>
<li>75% of female rape victims report that they were raped before age 25.</li>
<li>28% of male rape victims report that they were raped before age ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Risk factors vary among different ethnicities. About one third of African American adolescent females report encountering some form of sexual assault including rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> One in three Native American women will experience sexual assault, more than twice the national average for American women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2012_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2012-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>More than 250,000 cases of rape or attempted rape were recorded by <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police</a> annually in 65 countries in 2001 through 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In 2007, 40% of the 90,427 forcible rapes reported were cleared by arrest or "exceptional means." Exceptional refers to situations where the person refuses to provide information or assistance necessary to obtain an arrest, the defendant dies before being arrested, or the defendant cannot be <a href="/wiki/Extradition" title="Extradition">extradited</a> from another state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAOJ_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAOJ-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosecution">Prosecution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reporting">Reporting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_(photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg/220px-Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg/330px-Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg/440px-Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_%28photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangalore_protests_following_Delhi_gang-rape_(photo_-_Jim_Ankan_Deka).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
People in <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> demanding justice for young student who was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Delhi_gang_rape_case" title="2012 Delhi gang rape case">gang-raped in Delhi</a> in December 2012.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2005, sexual violence, and rape in particular, was considered the most under-reported violent crime in Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly2005_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly2005-116">[116]</a></sup> The number of reported rapes in Great Britain is lower than both incidence and prevalence rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The legal requirements for reporting rape vary by jurisdiction — each U.S. state may have different requirements.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> while one country, New Zealand has less stringent limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, a 2006 National Statistic Institute survey on sexual violence against women found that 91.6% of women who suffered this did not report it to the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conviction">Conviction</span></h3>
<p>In the United Kingdom, In 1970 there was a 33% rate of conviction, while by 1985 there was a 24 per cent conviction rate for rape trials in the UK, by 2004 the conviction rate reached 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> At that time the government report has expressed documented the year-on-year increase in attrition of reported rape cases, and pledged to address this "justice gap".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly2005_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly2005-116">[116]</a></sup> According to Amnesty International <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> had the lowest rate of conviction for rape, (1%) among 21 European states, in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In America as of 2012, there exists a noticeable discrepancy in conviction rates among women of various ethnic identities; an arrest was made in just 13% of the sexual assaults reported by American Indian women, compared with 35% for black women and 32% for whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2012_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2012-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="False_accusation">False accusation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/False_accusation_of_rape" title="False accusation of rape">False accusation of rape</a></div>
<p>Eight percent of 2,643 sexual assault cases were classified as false reports by the police in one study. The researchers noted that some of these classifications were based on the personal judgments of the police investigators and were made in violation of official criteria for establishing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_allegation" title="False allegation">false allegation</a>. Closer analysis of this category applying the Home Office counting rules for establishing a false allegation and excluding cases where the application of the cases where confirmation of the designation was uncertain reduced the percentage of false reports to 3%. The researchers concluded that "one cannot take all police designations at face value" and that "[t]here is an over-estimation of the scale of false allegations by both police officers and prosecutors." Moreover, they added:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The interviews with police officers and complainants' responses show that despite the focus on victim care, a culture of suspicion remains within the police, even amongst some of those who are specialists in rape investigations. There is also a tendency to conflate false allegations with retractions and withdrawals, as if in all such cases no sexual assault occurred. This reproduces an investigative culture in which elements that might permit a designation of a false complaint are emphasized (later sections reveal how this also feeds into withdrawals and designation of 'insufficient evidence'), at the expense of a careful investigation, in which the evidence collected is evaluated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sexual_assault:_key_issues_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sexual_assault:_key_issues-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Another large-scale study was conducted in Australia, with the 850 rapes reported to the Victoria police between 2000 and 2003 (Heenan &amp; Murray, 2006). Using both quantitative and qualitative methods, the researchers examined 812 cases with sufficient information to make an appropriate determination, and found that 2.1% of these were classified by police as false reports. All of these complainants were then charged or threatened with charges for filing a false police report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>FBI reports consistently put the number of "unfounded" rape accusations around 8%. The unfounded rate is higher for forcible rape than for any other Index crime. The average rate of unfounded reports for Index crimes is 2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> "Unfounded" is not synonymous with false allegation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and as Bruce Gross of the Forensic Examiner explains,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This statistic is almost meaningless, as many of the jurisdictions from which the FBI collects data on crime use different definitions of, or criteria for, "unfounded." That is, a report of rape might be classified as unfounded (rather than as forcible rape) if the alleged victim did not try to fight off the suspect, if the alleged perpetrator did not use physical force or a weapon of some sort, if the alleged victim did not sustain any physical injuries, or if the alleged victim and the accused had a prior sexual relationship. Similarly, a report might be deemed unfounded if there is no physical evidence or too many inconsistencies between the accuser's statement and what evidence does exist. As such, although some unfounded cases of rape may be false or fabricated, not all unfounded cases are false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAOJ_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-AAOJ-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Other studies have suggested that the rate of false allegations in America may be much higher. A nine-year study by Eugene J. Kanin of <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> in a small metropolitan area in the Midwestern United States found that 41% of rape accusations were false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-127">[127]</a></sup> However <a href="/wiki/David_Lisak" title="David Lisak">David Lisak</a>, an associate professor of psychology and director of the Men's Sexual Trauma Research Project at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Massachusetts_Boston" title="University of Massachusetts Boston">University of Massachusetts Boston</a> states that quote "Kanin's 1994 article on false allegations is a provocative opinion piece, but it is not a scientific study of the issue of false reporting of rape". He further states that Kanin's study has significantly poor systematic methodology and had no independent definition of a false report. Instead, Kanin classified reports that the police department classified as false also as false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VAW2_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-VAW2-128">[128]</a></sup> The criterion for falsehood was simply a denial of a polygraph test of the accuser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-127">[127]</a></sup> A 1998 report by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Justice" title="National Institute of Justice">National Institute of Justice</a> found that DNA evidence excluded the primary suspect in 26% of rape cases and concluded that this "strongly suggests that postarrest and postconviction DNA exonerations are tied to some strong, underlying systemic problems that generate erroneous accusations and convictions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 study by David Lisak, Lori Gardinier and other researchers published in the journal of <a href="/wiki/Violence_Against_Women_(journal)" title="Violence Against Women (journal)"><i>Violence against Women</i></a> found that out of 136 cases reported in a ten-year period, only 5.9% were found false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rape" title="History of rape">History of rape</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_evolution_of_laws">Definitions and evolution of laws</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="1350" height="450" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg/300px-Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg/450px-Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg/600px-Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Konstantin_Makovsky_-_The_Bulgarian_martyresses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Bulgarian martyresses</i>, a painting depicting the rape of Bulgarian women by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> troops during the <a href="/wiki/April_Uprising" title="April Uprising">April Uprising</a> of 1876.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> Caesar enacted reforms for the crime of rape under the assault statute <i>Lex Iulia de vi publica</i>, which bears his family name, <i>Iulia</i>. It was under this statute rather than the adultery statute of <i>Lex Iulia de adulteriis</i> that Rome prosecuted this crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In ancient Greece and Rome, both male-on-female and male-on-male concepts of rape existed. Roman laws allowed three distinct charges for the crime: <i>stuprum</i>, unsanctioned sexual intercourse (which, in the early times, also included adultery); <i>vis</i>, a physical assault for purpose of lust; and <i>iniuria</i>, a general charge denoting any type of assault upon person. Aforementioned <i>Lex Iulia</i> specifically criminalized <i>per vim stuprum</i>, unsanctioned sexual intercourse by force. The former two were public criminal charges which could be brought whenever the victim was a woman or a child of either gender, but only if the victim was a freeborn Roman citizen (<i><a href="/wiki/Ingenui" title="Ingenui">ingenuus</a></i>), and carried potential sentence of death or exile. <i>Iniuria</i> was a civil charge that demanded monetary compensation, and had a wider application (for example, it could have been brought in case of sexual assault on a slave by a person other than his/her owner.) In contrast to the modern understanding of the subject, Romans drew clear distinctions between "active" (penetrative) and "passive" (receptive) partners, and all these charges implied penetration by the assailant (which necessarily ruled out the possibility of female-on-male or female-on-female rape.) It is not clear which (if any) of these charges applied to assaults upon an adult male, though such an assault upon a citizen was definitely seen as a grave insult (within Roman culture, an adult male citizen could not possibly consent to the receptive role in a sexual intercourse without a severe loss of status.) The law known as <a href="/wiki/Lex_Scantinia" title="Lex Scantinia">Lex Scantinia</a> covered at least some forms of male-on-male <i>stuprum</i>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quintillian" title="Quintillian">Quintillian</a> mentions a fine of 10,000 sesterces – about 10 years worth of a Roman legionnaire's pay – as a normal penalty for <i>stuprum</i> upon an <i>ingenuus</i>. However, its text is lost and its exact provisions are no longer known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like theft or robbery, rape was originally considered a "private wrong" <i>iniuria privita</i>, a crime between the abductor and the legal guardian of the woman in question. It was made into a "public wrong" (<i>iniuria publica</i>) by the Roman Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a> continued the use of the statute to prosecute rape during the sixth century in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> By <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>, the general term <i>raptus</i> had referred to abduction, <a href="/wiki/Elopement" title="Elopement">elopement</a>, robbery, or rape in its modern meaning. Confusion over the term led ecclesiastical commentators on the law to differentiate it into <i>raptus seductionis</i> (elopement without parental consent) and <i>raptus violentiae</i> (ravishment). Both of these forms of <i>raptus</i> had a civil penalty and possible excommunication for the family and village receiving the abducted woman, although <i>raptus violentiae</i> also incurred punishments of mutilation or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Virtually all societies have had a concept of the crime of rape. Although what constituted this crime has varied by historical period and culture, until quite recently, the definitions tended to focus around an act of forced vaginal intercourse perpetrated through physical violence or imminent threat of death or severe bodily injury, by a man, on a woman or a girl, not his wife. These definitions differ significantly from the modern definitions of rape in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_countries" title="Western countries">Western countries</a> today. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Actus_reus" title="Actus reus">actus reus</a> of the crime, was, in most societies, the insertion of the penis into the vagina, and, until the 19th century, many jurisdictions required <a href="/wiki/Ejaculation" title="Ejaculation">ejaculation</a> for the act to constitute the offense of rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Acts other than vaginal intercourse did not constitute rape in <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> countries and in many other societies. In many cultures, such acts were illegal, even if they were consensual and performed between married couples (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodomy_laws" title="Sodomy laws">sodomy laws</a>). In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Buggery_Act_1533" title="Buggery Act 1533">Buggery Act 1533</a>, which remained in force until 1828, provided for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_penalty" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> for "<a href="/wiki/Buggery" title="Buggery">buggery</a>". Many countries criminalized "non-traditional" forms of sexual activity well into the modern era: notably, in the U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, sodomy between consensual partners was punishable by a term of five years to life in prison as late as 2003, and this law was only ruled to be inapplicable to married couples in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Today, in many countries, the definition of the actus reus has been extended to all forms of penetration of the vagina and anus (e.g. penetration with objects, fingers or other body parts) as well as insertion of the penis in the mouth.</p>
<p>Beyond the ancient era, due to the influence of Christianity on sexual mores, the notion of "rape" became narrowed to include only acts performed by a male to a female. The way sexuality was conceptualized in many societies rejected the very notion that a woman could force a man into sex — women were often seen as passive while men were deemed to be assertive and aggressive. Sexual penetration of a male by another male fell under the legal domain of "<a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">sodomy</a>".</p>
<p>One of the most distinctive features of rape historically was the fact that not all incidents of forced intercourse constituted this crime. An incident could be excluded from the definition of rape due to the relation between the parties, such as marriage (until a few decades ago wives were nearly universally excluded, and in many countries they continue to be so) or due to the background of the victim (in many cultures forced sex on prostitute, slave, war enemy, member of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_minority" title="Racial minority">racial minority</a>, etc., was not rape). For instance, in 17th century Scandinavia, Christian V's law of 1687 stipulated that rape could only be committed on three categories of women: somebody else's wife, a widow, or an honest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin" title="Virgin">virgin</a>. Women who did not fit in any of these categories were not considered legal victims of the crime of rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_issues">Social issues</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a>, rape was viewed less as a type of assault on the female, than a serious property crime against the man to whom she belonged, typically the father or husband. The loss of virginity was an especially serious matter. The damage due to loss of virginity was reflected in her reduced prospects in finding a husband and in her <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a>. This was especially true in the case of betrothed virgins, as the loss of <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">chastity</a> was perceived as severely depreciating her value to a prospective husband. In such cases, the law would void the betrothal and demand financial compensation from the rapist, payable to the woman's household, whose "goods" were "damaged".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Biblical_law" title="Biblical law">biblical law</a>, the rapist might be compelled to marry the unmarried woman instead of receiving the civil penalty if her father agreed. This was especially prevalent in laws where the crime of rape did not include, as a necessary element, the violation of the woman's body, thus dividing the crime in the current meaning of rape and a means for a man and woman to force their families to permit <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (January 2015)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup> (See <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblica.com/en-us/bible/online-bible/?osis=niv:Deuteronomy.22:28–29:28" rel="nofollow">Deuteronomy 22:28–29</a>.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary.2C_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (January 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>From the classical antiquity of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">Colonial period</a>, rape along with arson, <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a> and murder was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_offense" title="Capital offense">capital offense</a>. "Those committing rape were subject to a wide range of capital punishments that were seemingly brutal, frequently bloody, and at times spectacular." In the 12th century, kinsmen of the victim were given the option of executing the punishment themselves. "In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in the early fourteenth century, a victim of rape might be expected to gouge out the eyes and/or sever the offender's testicles herself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Despite the harshness of these laws, actual punishments were usually far less severe: in late Medieval Europe, cases concerning rapes of marriageable women, wives, widows, or members of the lower class were rarely brought forward, and usually ended with only a small monetary fine or a marriage between the victim and the rapist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eckman_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eckman-143">[143]</a></sup> In the 15 and 16th centuries, rape in the <a href="/wiki/Fuero#Basque_and_Pyrenean_fueros" title="Fuero">Basque charters</a> (<a href="/wiki/Enkarterri" title="Enkarterri">Enkarterri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durangaldea" title="Durangaldea">Durangaldea</a>) was punished with death for the offender and the person who was helping him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adult women were often extremely reluctant to bring up charges of rape: public admission of having been raped was severely damaging to one's social standing, courts tended to be skeptical of the charges, conviction rates were low, and, in the event that the accusation could not be proved, the victim could then be accused of committing adultery with the rapist (traditionally a serious offense that could have been punished by mutilation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> or even death). Certain classes of women, such as prostitutes, were banned from raising accusations of rape altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ius_primae_noctis" title="Ius primae noctis">ius primae noctis</a></i> ("law of the first night") is a term now popularly used to describe an alleged legal right allowing the lord of an estate to take the virginity of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">serfs</a>' maiden daughters. Little or no historical evidence has been unearthed from the Middle Ages to support the idea that such a right ever actually existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The medieval theologian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> argued that rape, though sinful, was much less sinful than <a href="/wiki/Masturbation" title="Masturbation">masturbation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coitus_interruptus" title="Coitus interruptus">coitus interruptus</a>, because it fulfilled the procreative function of sex, while the other acts violated the purpose of sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[notes 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Medieval Europe, a woman could be legally <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">married</a> by her parents to a stranger without her consent, and, once she was married, she could no longer refuse to consent to sex: the medieval concept of rape did not allow for the possibility of being raped by one's husband. In 1563, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> expressly declared that legal Catholic marriages had to be done with consent of both parties, but did not require parental consent, essentially declaring forced marriages invalid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> This was not universally accepted: for example, in France, women were not granted the right to marry without parental consent until 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>The criminal justice system of many countries was widely regarded as unfair to sexual assault victims. Both sexist stereotypes and common law combined to make rape a "criminal proceeding on which the victim and her behavior were tried rather than the defendant".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> Additionally, gender neutral laws have combated the older perception that rape never occurs to men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaFave_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaFave-156">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 1970s, many changes have occurred in the perception of sexual assault due in large part to the <a href="/wiki/Feminist_movement" title="Feminist movement">feminist movement</a> and its public characterization of rape as a crime of power and control rather than purely of sex. In some countries the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_liberation_movement" title="Women's liberation movement">women's liberation movement</a> of the 1970s created the first <a href="/wiki/Rape_crisis_center" title="Rape crisis center">rape crisis centers</a>. One of the first two rape crisis centers, the D.C. Rape Crisis Center, opened in 1972. It was created to promote sensitivity and understanding of rape and its effects on the victim. In 1960 law enforcement cited false reporting rates at 20%. By 1973 the statistics had dropped to 15%.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_war">In war</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_rape" title="War rape">War rape</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3118" data-file-width="4979" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%81ngel_DELLA_Valle_-_La_vuelta_del_mal%C3%B3n_-_Google_Art_Project_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>La vuelta del malón</i> (The return of the raiders) by <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ngel_Della_Valle" title="Ángel Della Valle">Ángel Della Valle</a> (1892).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1998, Judge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navanethem_Pillay" title="Navanethem Pillay">Navanethem Pillay</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda" title="International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda</a> said: "From time immemorial, rape has been regarded as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spoils_of_war" title="Spoils of war">spoils of war</a>. Now it will be considered a war crime. We want to send out a strong message that rape is no longer a trophy of war."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paulwalters_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-paulwalters-157">[156]</a></sup> Rape, in the course of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>, dates back to antiquity, ancient enough to have been mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women_in_the_Old_Testament_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Women_in_the_Old_Testament-158">[157]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Israel" title="Ancient Israel">Israelite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> armies reportedly engaged in war rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancient_origins:_Sexual_violence_in_warfare.2C_Part_I_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ancient_origins:_Sexual_violence_in_warfare.2C_Part_I-159">[158]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, who established the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> across much of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, caused <a href="/wiki/Destruction_under_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Destruction under the Mongol Empire">much destruction</a> during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mongol_conquests" title="Timeline of Mongol conquests">their invasions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Contemporary documents say that after a conquest, the Mongol soldiers looted, pillaged and raped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rogerius_of_Apulia" title="Rogerius of Apulia">Rogerius of Apulia</a>, a monk who survived the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Hungary" title="Mongol invasion of Hungary">Mongol invasion of Hungary</a>, the Mongol warriors "found pleasure" in humiliating local women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systematic_rape" title="Systematic rape">systematic rape</a> of as many as 80,000 women by the Japanese soldiers during the six weeks of the <a href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a> is an example of such atrocities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> an estimated 200,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_people" title="Korean people">Korean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese</a> women were forced into prostitution in <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japanese</a> military brothels, as so-called "<a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">Comfort women</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> French Moroccan troops known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goumier" title="Goumier">Goumiers</a> committed rapes and other war crimes after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monte_Cassino" title="Battle of Monte Cassino">Battle of Monte Cassino</a>. <i>(See <a href="/wiki/Marocchinate" title="Marocchinate">Marocchinate</a>.)</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> French women in Normandy complained about <a href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_liberation_of_France" title="Rape during the liberation of France">rapes during the liberation of Normandy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an20130529_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-an20130529-167">[166]</a></sup> Soldiers raping women and girls was common in many areas occupied by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Army_atrocities#World_War_II" title="Red Army atrocities">Red Army</a>. A female <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> war correspondent described what she had witnessed: "The Russian soldiers were raping every German female from eight to eighty. It was an army of rapists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-They_raped_every_German_female_from_eight_to_80_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-They_raped_every_German_female_from_eight_to_80-168">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to researcher and author <a href="/wiki/Kriszti%C3%A1n_Ungv%C3%A1ry" title="Krisztián Ungváry">Krisztián Ungváry</a>, some 38,000 <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civilians</a> were killed during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Budapest" title="Siege of Budapest">Siege of Budapest</a>: about 13,000 from military action and 25,000 from starvation, disease and other causes. Included in the latter figure are about 15,000 Jews, largely victims of executions by Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Arrow_Cross_Party" title="Arrow Cross Party">Arrow Cross Party</a> militia. When the Soviets finally claimed victory, they initiated an orgy of violence, including the wholesale theft of anything they could lay their hands on, random executions and mass rape. An estimated 50,000 women and girls were raped,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ungvary_512_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ungvary_512-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:348–350</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rape_group.3Dnotes_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-rape_group.3Dnotes-171">[notes 2]</a></sup> although estimates vary from 5,000 to 200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:129</sup> Hungarian girls were kidnapped and taken to Red Army quarters, where they were imprisoned, repeatedly raped and sometimes murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naimark_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naimark-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:70–71</sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Aquinas, <i>Summa Theologica</i>, question 154. "...it follows that in this matter [the sin of unnatural vice] is gravest of all. After it comes incest... With regard to the other species of lust they imply a transgression merely of that which is determined by right reason, on the presupposition, however, of natural principles." Here, <i>unnatural vice</i> includes, in decreasing order of sinfulness, bestiality, homosexual intercourse, any unconventional form of heterosexual intercourse, and 'uncleanness' (masturbation). 'Other species of lust' include intercourse with a <a href="/wiki/Consecrated_virgin" title="Consecrated virgin">consecrated virgin</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nun</a>, rape, adultery, seduction of a virgin, and 'simple fornication' (consensual intercourse between unmarried people.)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-rape_group.3Dnotes-171"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-rape_group.3Dnotes_171-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The worst suffering of the Hungarian population is due to the rape of women. Rapes—affecting all age groups from ten to seventy are so common that very few women in Hungary have been spared." Swiss embassy report cited in Ungváry 2005, p.350. (Krisztian Ungvary <i>The Siege of Budapest</i> 2005)</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a><a class="external text" href="https://www.hrw.org/legacy/reports/2001/prison/report7.html#_1_48" rel="nofollow">No Escape: Male Rape In U.S. Prisons. Quoted in <cite class="citation book">Mariner, Joanne; (Organization), Human Rights Watch (2001-04-17). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=QkFfYfEO5IgC&amp;pg=PA370" rel="nofollow"><i>No escape: male rape in U.S. prisons</i></a>. Human Rights Watch. p. 370. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-56432-258-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-56432-258-6">978-1-56432-258-6</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 June</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARape&amp;rft.au=%28Organization%29%2C+Human+Rights+Watch&amp;rft.aufirst=Joanne&amp;rft.aulast=Mariner&amp;rft.btitle=No+escape%3A+male+rape+in+U.S.+prisons&amp;rft.date=2001-04-17&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQkFfYfEO5IgC%26pg%3DPA370&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-56432-258-6&amp;rft.pages=370&amp;rft.pub=Human+Rights+Watch&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Struckman-Johnson, Cindy; David Struckman-Johnson (2006). "A Comparison of Sexual Coercion Experiences Reported by Men and Women in Prison". <i>Journal of Interpersonal Violence</i>. <b>21</b> (12): 1591–1615. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1177%2F0886260506294240" rel="nofollow">10.1177/0886260506294240</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0886-2605" rel="nofollow">0886-2605</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17065656" rel="nofollow">17065656</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARape&amp;rft.atitle=A+Comparison+of+Sexual+Coercion+Experiences+Reported+by+Men+and+Women+in+Prison&amp;rft.au=David+Struckman-Johnson&amp;rft.aufirst=Cindy&amp;rft.aulast=Struckman-Johnson&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F0886260506294240&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F17065656&amp;rft.issn=0886-2605&amp;rft.issue=12&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;rft.pages=1591-1615&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=21"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>; reports that "Greater percentages of men (70%) than women (29%) reported that their incident resulted in oral, vaginal, or anal sex. <li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Michigan Statutes for the first degree felony, section 520b, "(1) A person is guilty of criminal sexual conduct in the first degree if he or she engages in sexual penetration of another person.", or in England and Wales, Section 1 of the Sexual Offences Act 2003 "1. A person (A) commits an offence if – (a) he intentionally penetrates the vagina, anus or mouth of another person..." – although in this case women are still not capable of committing rape.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Psych2013rev-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Psych2013rev_57-11"><sup><i><b>l</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Mason, F; Lodrick, Z (February 2013). "Psychological consequences of sexual assault.". <i>Best practice &amp; research. Clinical obstetrics &amp; gynaecology</i>. <b>27</b> (1): 27–37. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.bpobgyn.2012.08.015" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.bpobgyn.2012.08.015</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23182852" rel="nofollow">23182852</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARape&amp;rft.atitle=Psychological+consequences+of+sexual+assault.&amp;rft.aufirst=F&amp;rft.aulast=Mason&amp;rft.au=Lodrick%2C+Z&amp;rft.date=2013-02&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.bpobgyn.2012.08.015&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23182852&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.jtitle=Best+practice+%26+research.+Clinical+obstetrics+%26+gynaecology&amp;rft.pages=27-37&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=27"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note: One of the authors of the "Psychological consequences of sexual assault" article describes what she means by "friend" and "flop" in an article: <cite class="citation journal">Lodrick, Zoe (2007). <a class="external text" href="http://www.zoelodrick.co.uk/training/article-1" rel="nofollow">"Psychological trauma – what every trauma worker should know."</a>. <i>The British Journal of Psychotherapy Integration</i>. <b>4</b> (2).</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARape&amp;rft.atitle=Psychological+trauma+%93+what+every+trauma++worker+should+know.&amp;rft.aufirst=Zoe&amp;rft.aulast=Lodrick&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.zoelodrick.co.uk%2Ftraining%2Farticle-1&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=The+British+Journal+of+Psychotherapy+Integration.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=4"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Friend: "Friend is the earliest defensive strategy available to us..... Throughout life when fearful most humans will activate their social engagement system (Porges, 1995). ... The social engagement system, or friend response to threat, is evident in the child who smiles or even laughs when being chastised." Flop: "Flop occurs if, and when, the freeze mechanism fails.... The survival purpose of the flop state is evident: if ‘impact’ is going to occur the likelihood of surviving it will be increased if the body yields, and psychologically, in the short-term at least, the situation will be more bearable if the higher brain functions are ‘offline’."</span></li>
